## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: solid mechanics / materials science (thermo-mechanical constitutive modeling)
**Analysis**: The variables are stress (sigma), strain (epsilon), and temperature (T), which strongly indicates a constitutive law for a solid under thermo-mechanical loading. Typical relationships include elastic stress–strain laws, temperature-dependent modulus/yield stress, and thermo-activated plastic/viscoplastic flow rules.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Linear thermoelasticity (temperature-dependent modulus): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon (elastic regime with modulus softening/stiffening vs T)
- Nonlinear elastic polynomial: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon + (c2 + c3 * T) * epsilon**2 (moderate strains, empirical elastic nonlinearity)
- Hyperelastic (exponential stiffening): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * (exp(c2 * epsilon) - 1) (rubber-like or fitted stiffening curves with T effects)
- Hyperelastic (logarithmic form): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * log(1 + c2 * epsilon) (compressible/incompressible-inspired fits; diminishing slope at larger strain)
- Power-law hardening with temperature-dependent strength: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * (epsilon + c2)**c3 (plastic/total flow curve with offset strain and T sensitivity)
- Ludwik-type hardening (additive yield + hardening): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) + (c2 + c3 * T) * epsilon**c4 (yield stress plus strain hardening; temperature softening via coefficients)
- Ramberg–Osgood-style implicit/explicit fit (stress as function of strain): sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * epsilon**c2 (combined elastic + nonlinear/plastic part; can extend coefficients to c(T))
- Johnson–Cook-like multiplicative structure (strain hardening × thermal softening): sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) * (1 - (c3 * T)**c4) (empirical plasticity with thermal softening factor)
- Thermal softening via rational factor: sigma = (c0 * epsilon**c1) / (1 + c2 * T**c3) (strength decreases with T; avoids singularities)
- Coupled cross-term expansion: sigma = c0 + c1 * epsilon + c2 * T + c3 * epsilon * T (first-order thermo-mechanical coupling / local linearization)
- Exponential thermal softening of modulus/strength: sigma = c0 * epsilon * exp(-c1 * T) (rapid degradation of stiffness/strength with temperature)
- Sigmoidal temperature transition (e.g., glass transition-like softening): sigma = (c0 * epsilon) * (1 - 1 / (1 + exp(-c1 * (T - c2)))) (sharp drop in stiffness across a transition temperature)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Elastic regime identification: near-origin linear dependence sigma ∝ epsilon suggests Hookean elasticity; deviations indicate plasticity or nonlinear elasticity terms (epsilon**2, exp(c*epsilon), log(1+c*epsilon)).
- Temperature softening of stiffness/strength: factors like (c0 + c1 * T), exp(-c*T), or 1/(1+c*T**m) represent decreasing modulus/yield stress as T rises.
- Strain hardening behavior: sigma increasing sublinearly/superlinearly with epsilon is captured by epsilon**n, (epsilon+c)**n, or additive yield-plus-hardening forms.
- Transition temperatures: sharp changes in slope vs T (e.g., around glass transition or phase changes) are well represented by logistic/sigmoid functions in T.
- Coupled thermo-mechanical interaction: mixed term epsilon * T (or epsilon**n * T**m) indicates that temperature changes the strain sensitivity (hardening rate or modulus).
- Avoiding nonphysical negative stress: thermal softening factors like (1 - (c*T)**m) require bounds; rational or exponential-in-T forms can enforce positivity more naturally.
- Low-strain regularization: offsets (epsilon + c) inside powers/logs prevent singular derivatives at epsilon = 0 and often improve fit to yield-onset behavior.
- Asymptotic stiffness/strength limits: log(1+c*epsilon) gives diminishing incremental stiffness; exp(c*epsilon) gives rapidly increasing stiffness—useful cues for material class (soft polymers vs strain-stiffening networks).

GT (fixed) can be rewritten to expose its functional “basis”:
\[
\text{GT}=11.1190537583\,\epsilon^2 \;+\;11.1190537583\,\epsilon\big(-0.0919563017\,(T-352.0084637)+1\big)\;+\;4.3699183275\,(T-352.0084637)\log(\epsilon+1).
\]
Expand the \(\epsilon\) term:
\[
11.119\,\epsilon\big(-0.091956\,T + 0.091956\cdot352.008 + 1\big)
= (\alpha)\,T\epsilon + (\beta)\,\epsilon
\]
with \(\alpha\neq 0\). Also
\[
4.3699\,(T-352.008)\log(\epsilon+1)= (4.3699)\,T\log(\epsilon+1) + (\text{const})\log(\epsilon+1).
\]
So GT is exactly a linear combination of these independent components:
- \(\epsilon^2\)
- \(T\epsilon\)
- \(\epsilon\)
- \(T\log(\epsilon+1)\)
- \(\log(\epsilon+1)\)

and **no other** dependence (no \(\epsilon\log\), no denominators, no exponentials/sines, no \(T\epsilon^2\), etc.).
